Estimation of spectral gaps for sparse symmetric matrices
Michele Benzi1 ┬╖ Michele Rinelli2 ┬╖ Igor Simunec3
Received: 22 October 2024 / Revised: 8 September 2025 / Accepted: 17 January 2026 /
Published online: 26 February 2026
┬⌐ The Author(s) 2026, modi∩¼üed publication 2026
Abstract
In this paper we propose and analyze an algorithm for identifying spectral gaps
of a real symmetric matrix A by simultaneously approximating the traces of spec-
tral projectors associated with multiple different spectral slices. Our method utilizes
HutchinsonΓÇÖs stochastic trace estimator together with the Lanczos algorithm to approx-
imatequadraticformsinvolvingspectralprojectors.Insteadoffocusingondetermining
the gap between two particular consecutive eigenvalues of A, we aim to ∩¼ünd all gaps
that are wider than a speci∩¼üed threshold. By examining the problem from this per-
spective, and thoroughly analyzing both the Hutchinson and the Lanczos components
of the algorithm, we obtain error bounds that allow us to determine the number of
HutchinsonΓÇÖs sample vectors and Lanczos iterations needed to ensure the detection of
all gaps above the target width with high probability. In particular, we conclude that
the most ef∩¼ücient strategy is to always use a single random sample vector for Hutchin-
sonΓÇÖs estimator and concentrate all computational effort in the Lanczos algorithm. Our
numerical experiments demonstrate the ef∩¼üciency and reliability of this approach.
Mathematics Subject Classi∩¼ücation 65F50 ┬╖ 65F15
1 Introduction
We consider the problem of locating gaps in the spectrum of an n ├ù n real symmetric
matrix A, i.e., ∩¼ünding intervals that do not contain any eigenvalue of A. A variant of
this problem that is more commonly encountered in the literature is the following:

given an integer k with 1 Γëñk < n, ∩¼ünd a real number ╬╝ such that exactly k of the
eigenvalues of A (counted with their multiplicities) are strictly below ╬╝. This problem
can be equivalently restated as ∩¼ünding ╬╝ such that the spectral projector P╬╝ onto the
subspace spanned by the eigenvectors of A associated with eigenvalues strictly smaller
than ╬╝ satis∩¼ües rank(P╬╝) = k; since for a projector the rank is equal to the trace, we
can also write this requirement as tr(P╬╝) = k. If we denote the eigenvalues of A
by ╬╗i and we label them in non-decreasing order, this problem has a solution only if
╬╗k < ╬╗k+1 (non-degeneracy assumption). In this case, any ╬╝ lying in the open interval
(╬╗k, ╬╗k+1) is a solution. Instead of looking for the gap for a speci∩¼üc value of k, the
approach that we present here aims to ∩¼ünd all gaps in the spectrum with width above a
certain threshold, and in addition we obtain an estimate of the number of eigenvalues
below each gap. In practical cases where it is of interest to ∩¼ünd ╬╝ in the gap between
╬╗k and ╬╗k+1, the gap (╬╗k, ╬╗k+1) is often relatively large, so we expect to be able to
∩¼ünd it by looking for all gaps above a certain width.
There are several applications where it is required to locate gaps in the spectrum of
a given symmetric matrix. In KohnΓÇôSham Density Functional Theory for electronic
structure computations [1], at each step of a self-consistent ∩¼üeld iteration it is necessary
tocomputeeithertheeigenvectorsofasymmetricmatrix(thelinearizeddiscreteKohnΓÇô
Sham Hamiltonian) associated with eigenvalues below the Fermi level (or chemical
potential, usually denoted by ╬╝), corresponding to the so-called occupied states of
the system, or equivalently the corresponding spectral projector P╬╝. In the case of
insulators at zero electronic temperature, the linearized Hamiltonians exhibit a gap
separating the ∩¼ürst k eigenvalues from the rest of the spectrum, where k is the number
of electrons, and locating this gap is equivalent to estimating the Fermi level ╬╝. In
particular, in linear scaling electronic structure computations the spectral projector is
approximated either by polynomials or by rational functions that act as ∩¼ülters, i.e.,
approximate the step function h╬╝(╬╗) that takes the value 1 for ╬╗ < ╬╝ and 0 for ╬╗ > ╬╝
(see, e.g., [2]). Clearly, in order to use this approach it is ∩¼ürst necessary to estimate
the Fermi level ╬╝, which is a nontrivial task.
Another potential area of application arises in solid state physics, where the loca-
tion of gaps in the energy spectrum of quantum-mechanical systems described by
Schr├╢dinger operators with periodic potentials is an important problem; see, e.g., [3],
as well as [4].
An additional possible area of application is spectral clustering, which uses the
eigenvectors of the graph Laplacian to partition a graph, see [5] and references therein.
The stability of the partitioning under small perturbations of the weights is governed
by the gaps in the spectrum of the graph Laplacian; in particular, relatively large
gaps correspond to stable partitions. Hence, identifying suf∩¼üciently large gaps in the
spectrum of the Laplacian matrix is an important preliminary step for any spectral
clustering algorithm.
Finally, as a further application we mention estimating the stability of invariant sub-
spaces under perturbations. It is well known in numerical linear algebra that individual
eigenvectors of symmetric matrices corresponding to a cluster of tightly spaced eigen-
values will be highly ill-conditioned. On the other hand, if the cluster is well-separated
from the rest of the spectrum, the invariant subspace spanned by those eigenvectors
is well-conditioned and can be determined with high accuracy; furthermore, iterative
123


--- PAGE 3 ---
Estimation of spectral gaps for sparse symmetric matrices
849
methods designed to approximate the invariant subspace (or, equivalently, the orthog-
onal projector onto it) will typically converge rapidly. Hence, being able to ef∩¼üciently
identify gaps in the spectrum of a symmetric matrix A provides a way to estimate
the conditioning of invariant subspaces. Moreover, when A is banded, or sparse, the
invariant space is ΓÇ£localizedΓÇ¥, an important property that can reduce computational
costs considerably; see, e.g., [6] and also [2, Sec. 11.4] and [7].
In the literature, a few approaches have been proposed to estimate values of ╬╝ lying
within the target gap. It is well known that for any real symmetric matrix AΓêÆ╬╝I there
exists a unit lower triangular matrix L and a block diagonal matrix D with 1 ├ù 1 and
2 ├ù 2 blocks such that A ΓêÆ╬╝I = L DLT ; see for example [8, Chap. 1.3.4]. Since A
and D are congruent, they have the same inertia, i.e., the same number of positive,
negative, and possibly zero eigenvalues (the latter can occur only when ╬╝ happens
to be an eigenvalue of A, an extremely unlikely occurrence in practice). Hence, if A
is not too large, an L DLT factorization of A ΓêÆ╬╝I can be used to count the number
of eigenvalues smaller than ╬╝. If this number is less than k (larger than k) then ╬╝
is increased (respectively, decreased) and the procedure is repeated until the correct
value is found. When A is sparse, one can make use of symmetric row and column
permutations to reduce the ∩¼üll-in in L (and therefore the arithmetic and storage costs)
while preserving sparsity, see [9, Chap. 7]. A major drawback of this approach is its
cost, and especially the fact that having computed the L DLT factorization of A ΓêÆ╬╝I
for a certain ╬╝ is of no help in computing the factorization for a different value of
╬╝, hence using matrix factorizations in a trial-and-error fashion can be prohibitively
expensive, even assuming that a factorization can be computed at all.
Differenttechniqueshavebeenusedinthecontextoflinearscalingmethods.Inthese
methods, an initial guess ╬╝0 Γëê╬╝ is used in constructing a polynomial approximation
totheprojector P╬╝0 byaniterativeprocessknownaspuri∩¼ücation,essentiallyaNewton-
type method. The value of ╬╝ is adjusted in the course of the iterative process until
the prescribed value k of the trace is obtained; hence, the determination of ╬╝ and the
approximation of the projector P╬╝ are interweaved. The main cost in this approach is
the need to repeatedly solve n ├ù n linear systems with variable coef∩¼ücient matrix at
each iteration. We refer to [10, 11] for details on these adaptive procedures.
Yet another approach is the one that combines stochastic trace estimators with
the Lanczos algorithm to compute an approximation of tr(P╬╝). An advantage of this
approach is that we can exploit the shift-invariance property of Krylov subspaces (see,
e.g., [12]) to ef∩¼üciently approximate the trace of the spectral projectors P╬╝ associated
with many different values of ╬╝ at the same time. The combination of HutchinsonΓÇÖs
estimator and the Lanczos algorithm has also appeared in literature on related prob-
lems that exploit the trace of spectral projectors, such as the estimation of the number
of eigenvalues of a matrix A contained in a given interval [a, b] or the estimation of
spectral densities, see, for instance, [13ΓÇô16]. In the context of spectral density approx-
imation, a similar approach is the kernel polynomial method, which was introduced
in [17, 18] and uses a polynomial expansion based on Chebyshev polynomials.
Here we focus on the approach that combines HutchinsonΓÇÖs trace estimator and
the Lanczos algorithm to estimate the trace of the spectral projectors P╬╝. When using
this kind of approach, it is essential to have a criterion to choose the parameters of
the algorithm, namely the number of random vectors xi for HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator
123


--- PAGE 4 ---
850
M. Benzi et al.
and the number of Lanczos iterations, in order to minimize the computational cost
and guarantee the correctness of the results. However, although there exist theoretical
results on the convergence speed of this approach in the literature, they are mainly
focusedontheglobalapproximationofthespectraldensityandtheyarenotsatisfactory
when applied to gap detection; in particular, they are not useful to determine how the
parameters should be selected. The main purpose of this work is to answer this question
for the problem of ∩¼ünding gaps in the spectrum of a matrix. By changing the point
of view from ∩¼ünding ╬╝ such that the number of eigenvalues below ╬╝ is exactly k to
∩¼ünding all the gaps in the spectrum with width above a certain threshold, we are able to
provide a rigorous analysis that allows us to choose the input parameters to ensure that
all the target gaps are found, and at the same time minimize the computational cost. Our
analysis will determine that for this problem the most ef∩¼ücient choice is to use a single
quadratic form xT P╬╝x to approximate tr(P╬╝), but in order to reach this conclusion
we ∩¼ürst have to analyze the more general algorithm that uses HutchinsonΓÇÖs stochastic
trace estimator. This analysis will result in an algorithm that simultaneously computes
upper and lower bounds to xT P╬╝x for all considered values of ╬╝, and exploits them
to ∩¼ünd intervals that contain no eigenvalues of A with high probability.
The rest of the paper is organized as follows. In Sect.2 we describe a basic version
of the algorithm that we are going to use to detect the gaps in the spectrum of A.
Each component of the algorithm is then analyzed in detail in Sect.3, with a focus
on bounding the error. Then, in Sect.4 we put together the analysis to determine the
best choice of input parameters and we obtain an ef∩¼ücient and robust algorithm that
can give guarantees on its output. In Sect.5 we conduct some numerical experiments
to evaluate the performance and reliability of our approach. Finally, Sect.6 contains
some concluding remarks.

2.1 HutchinsonΓÇÖs trace estimator
Stochastic trace estimators approximate the trace of a matrix B that is accessible via
matrixΓÇôvector products by making use of the fact that, for any random vector x such
that E[xxT ] = I, we have E[xT Bx] = tr(B), where E denotes the expected value.
HutchinsonΓÇÖs trace estimator [19] is a simple stochastic estimator that generates s
vectors x1, . . . , xs with i.i.d. random entries, that are usually chosen as either Gaussian
N(0, 1) or Rademacher (uniform ┬▒1), and approximates tr(B) with

handle the case where ╬╝ is near the center of the spectrum. To overcome this issue, we
will have to slightly change our perspective in order to develop an ef∩¼ücient algorithm.
This topic will be discussed in greater detail in Sect.3.2.
Remark 2.2 Since rank(P╬╝) = ne(╬╝) and all the eigenvalues of P╬╝ are either 0 or 1,
for this setting it usually does not make much sense to use the improved stochastic
trace estimators such as Hutch++ [22, 23] or XTrace [24], which also employ a low-
rank matrix approximation. Indeed, there is no gradual decay in the eigenvalues of P╬╝
and hence there is no advantage in computing a low-rank approximation of P╬╝, unless
ne(╬╝) is very small compared to the matrix size.
Remark 2.3 When the matrix A is sparse, we could alternatively approximate the trace
of the matrix function P╬╝ = h╬╝(A) with a probing method (see, for instance, [25ΓÇô
27]). The probing approach has a faster convergence when the function h╬╝(A) is
well-approximated by polynomials on the spectrum of A [25, Theorem 4.4], i.e., it
converges similarly to the Lanczos method for the computation of h╬╝(A)x. We will
see in Sect.3.3 that this convergence rate is heavily dependent on the value of ╬╝, so
we prefer to use a stochastic trace estimator in order to eliminate this dependence.

2.3 Simultaneous computation for different 
The approach described above can be easily modi∩¼üed in order to compute approxima-
tions to tr(P╬╝) simultaneously for several ╬╝, for a cost that is only slightly higher than
the cost of the approximation for a single ╬╝. The key observation is that for a ∩¼üxed
matrix A andvector x,wecanextractapproximationstothequadraticforms xT f j(A)x
for several different functions f j from a single Krylov subspace Km(A, x). Since the
cost of computing the approximation (2.2) is dominated by the cost of constructing the
orthonormal basis Vm via the Lanczos algorithm, the additional operations required
to compute approximations for different functions represent only a minor part of the
overall cost. More precisely, let us consider N f different functions f j, j = 1, . . . , N f .
For a ∩¼üxed Krylov subspace dimension m, we can approximate each quadratic form
xT f j(A)x with
qm, j := ΓêÑxΓêÑ2
2eT

j = 1, . . . , N f .
The quantities qm, j can be computed ef∩¼üciently in the following way. Obtain ∩¼ürst an
eigenvalue decomposition Tm = Um DmU T

by applying the functions f j to the diagonal entries of Dm. The Lanczos approxima-
tions to the quadratic forms can now be computed with the identity

that we have to do when we have N f different functions are the computation of the
diagonal matrix functions f j(Dm) and the computation of the ∩¼ünal approximations

negligiblecomparedtothe O(m nnz(A))costforthematrixΓÇôvectorproductswith A in
the construction of the Krylov subspace Km(A, x), unless the number of functions N f
is extremely large.
In total, the cost of computing approximations to xT f j(A)x for j = 1, . . . , N f
with the Lanczos algorithm adds up to O(m nnz(A) + nm + m2 + mN f ), where
the O(nm) term is the cost of orthogonalization in the Lanczos algorithm using the
three-term recurrence; if full reorthogonalization is used instead to increase stability,
the orthogonalization cost would increase to O(nm2). The O(m2) term corresponds
to the computation of eigenvalues and eigenvectors1 of the tridiagonal matrix Tm,
and the O(mN f ) term corresponds to the computation of qm, j for all j. Note that
the convergence rate of the approximation of the quadratic form xT f j(A)x can vary
signi∩¼ücantly depending on the function f j, so this approach may construct approxi-
mations with considerably different errors for different functions, since we are using
1 The O(m2) cost for computing eigenvectors of a tridiagonal matrix assumes that the MRRR algorithm
[30, 31] is used, which is now the state of the art in SCALAPACK and other software packages. We note
that this algorithm might fail in some extreme cases; however, remedies are known [32].

Remark 2.4 A similar approach could be used to approximate quadratic forms with
several different functions using rational Krylov methods [33], which depend on a
certain sequence of poles ╬╛ = [╬╛1, . . . , ╬╛mΓêÆ1] and employ as an approximation space
the rational Krylov subspace Qm(A, x, ╬╛) = qmΓêÆ1(A)ΓêÆ1Km(A, b), with qmΓêÆ1(z) :=
mΓêÆ1
j=1 (z ΓêÆ╬╛ j). However, the convergence of a rational Krylov method is heavily
dependent on the poles ╬╛, which should be chosen depending on the function. In
particular, in the case of f j = h╬╝ j for different parameters ╬╝ j, a sequence of poles
that produces an accurate approximation for a certain ╬╝ j will give poor approximations
for other parameters, especially if they are far from ╬╝ j. For this reason, the Lanczos
method is better suited for this scenario. A rational Krylov subspace method could be
useful to compute a more accurate approximation for the quadratic form xT h╬╝(A)x
associated with a speci∩¼üc parameter ╬╝, especially in situations in which the Lanczos
method converges very slowly.
Returning now to the approximation of tr(P╬╝) with HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator, let us
consider N f different parameters {╬╝ j}
N f
j=1 ΓèéR and de∩¼üne the functions f j = h╬╝ j .
Provided that we use the same random sample vectors {xi}s
i=1 for the approximation
of tr(P╬╝ j ) via HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator for all j, we can use the same Krylov subspace
Km(A, xi) to extract approximations to xT
i P╬╝ j xi for all ╬╝ j at the same time, with
essentially the same cost of approximating a quadratic form for a single parameter ╬╝.
This feature of this approach represents a considerable computational advantage over
other methods, such as those based on the L DLT factorization, which are only able
to test a single candidate ╬╝ at a time.
A high-level algorithmic description of the procedure outlined in this section is
given in Algorithm 1. This algorithm returns an approximation to tr(P╬╝ j ) for all
j = 1, . . . , N f . The level of accuracy of these approximations, as well as whether
they can be exploited to detect gaps in the spectrum of A, is going to be thoroughly
investigated in the sections that follow. We are also going to identify criteria for
selecting the input parameters of the method, namely the number of Hutchinson sample
vectors s and Lanczos iterations m. The product of this analysis will be an algorithm
that, given as input a failure probability ╬┤ and a relative gap width ╬╕, aims to ∩¼ünd all
the gaps in the spectrum whose relative width is larger than ╬╕; each one of these gaps
is found with probability at least 1ΓêÆ╬┤. In addition, all the gaps found by the algorithm
are certi∩¼üed, in the sense that the probability of ∩¼ünding an eigenvalue of A within a
gap is smaller than ╬┤.
Remark 2.5 We mention that Algorithm 1 has been extensively used in the literature
for related problems, such as spectral density estimation and estimation of eigenvalue
counts in an interval [13, 15, 16], and it is also known as ΓÇ£stochastic Lanczos quadra-
tureΓÇ¥ [16]. The main novelty of our work is in the theoretical analysis contained in
the following sections, which provides guarantees for the detection of spectral gaps,
and allows us to select the parameters s and m in order to minimize the computational
cost. We are going to provide a more detailed comparison of our contributions with
those found in the literature in Sect.4.5.

Algorithm 1 Eigenvalue gap ∩¼ünder ΓÇô Prototype
Input: Symmetric matrix A ΓêêRn├ùn, number of Hutchinson samples s, Krylov subspace dimension m,
vector of test parameters ╬╝ = [╬╝1, . . . , ╬╝N f ]
Output: tr j Γëêtr(P╬╝ j ) for j = 1, . . . , N f
1: for i = 1, . . . , s do
2:
Draw a vector xi ΓêêRn with random i.i.d. Gaussian N(0, 1) entries
3:

3 Algorithm analysis
In this section we examine some general properties of the trace approximations com-
puted by Algorithm 1, and we analyze in detail the errors that occur in the Hutchinson
and Lanczos parts of the approximation.

Example 3.1 To illustrate the behavior of the approximation of tr(P╬╝) computed by
Algorithm 1 on a simple example, we consider a symmetric matrix A of size n = 600,
with eigenvalues contained in the interval [0, 60] and three gaps given by the intervals
[20, 21], [30, 32] and [40, 44]. In Fig.1 we compare the approximation from Algo-

s (P╬╝) with exact
quadratic forms, and the exact trace tr(P╬╝), which coincides with ne(╬╝), for several
different values of ╬╝. The number of random vectors is either s = 1 or s = 10.
Figure1 con∩¼ürms the behavior described above. In particular, we see that Hutchin-
sonΓÇÖs estimator with exact quadratic forms has a jump whenever ╬╝ coincides with an
eigenvalue of A, so it correctly detects all the gaps in the spectrum, although the value
of the approximation of tr(P╬╝) can be quite off, especially with s = 1. On the other
hand, HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator combined with the Lanczos algorithm for computing
quadratic forms, which is the approximation that we are actually able to compute, has
jumps when ╬╝ corresponds to an eigenvalue of one of the projected matrices T (i)
m . We
see that the Lanczos approximation is able to detect the largest gap quite well, but the
other two gaps do not stand out from the other steps in the staircase-like curve. With
s = 10, the Lanczos curve looks like a staircase with somewhat jagged steps, because
the projected matrices T (i)
m have different eigenvalues for different random vectors xi,
and the curve is obtained by averaging the approximations q(i)
m, j associated with each
T (i)
m . Note that the curves in Fig.1 are approximations of the (unnormalized) spectral
density of A, which coincide with the approximations shown, for instance, in [16,
Fig. 1].

Remark 3.1 In alternative to Gaussian random vectors, we can also use Rademacher

We can combine this result with (3.4) to bound the error in the Lanczos approximation

gap (╬╗ne(╬╝), ╬╗ne(╬╝)+1). Unfortunately, we cannot exclude a priori that an eigenvalue of
Tm lies within the gap, and indeed this often happens in practice; however, since there
must be at least one eigenvalue of A in the open interval between two consecutive
Ritz values [37, Sect. 4], there can be at most one eigenvalue of Tm in the interval
(╬╗ne(╬╝), ╬╗ne(╬╝)+1). This fact can be proved using the theory of orthogonal polynomials,
see, for instance, [38, Theorem 1.21], using the fact that the Ritz values are the zeros
of an orthogonal polynomial associated with a Gauss quadrature rule; see, e.g., [29,
Theorem 6.2]. In the analysis that follows, we assume for simplicity that there are no
Ritz values in the gap (╬╗ne(╬╝), ╬╗ne(╬╝)+1). We discuss how we can modify our approach
to deal with this issue in Remark 3.7.
Remark 3.5 In the literature, several techniques have been used to obtain approxima-
tions to the sign matrix function that circumvent the problem of Ritz values getting
arbitrarily close to zero [39], for instance by writing sign(z) = z ┬╖ (z2)ΓêÆ1/2 and con-
structing an approximation of (A2)ΓêÆ1/2x using the Krylov subspace Km(A2, x), which
ensures that the eigenvalues of the projected matrix are bounded away from 0, since
A2 is positive de∩¼ünite as long as A is nonsingular. Alternatively, one can construct an
approximation using harmonic Ritz values [40], which are always outside of the inter-
val between the largest negative and the smallest positive eigenvalue of A, so they avoid
the discontinuity of the sign function. Since in this work our goal is to approximate
xT h╬╝(A)x for several ╬╝, we opted to use the standard Lanczos approximation (2.2),
for which it is very cheap and straightforward to compute approximations for many
different functions.
Combining (3.4) with (3.5), we obtain the following bound on the Lanczos approx-
imation error.

A crucial requirement for using the bound in Proposition 3.6 is the knowledge of
the relative gap width ╬╕. Since the gaps in the spectrum are not known to us in advance,
we cannot use this result to check if the error of the Lanczos method is below a certain
tolerance ╬╡. On the other hand, Proposition 3.6 can be useful to choose the number
m of Lanczos iterations at the start of the algorithm. Indeed, if our goal is to ∩¼ünd all
the gaps in the spectrum of A with relative width at least ╬╕, for any given ╬╡ we can
select m using (3.8) to ensure that the error |xT h╬╝(A)x ΓêÆqm| Γëñ╬╡ whenever ╬╝ is
inside a gap with width at least ╬╕. However, we are still unable to determine for which
values of ╬╝ this condition is satis∩¼üed. The ∩¼ünal component that we need to make the
algorithm work is an a posteriori bound on the error, which will allow us to detect
when |xT h╬╝(A)x ΓêÆqm| Γëñ╬╡, without knowing the gaps in the spectrum in advance.
For this purpose we can use an a posteriori error bound from [26], which we adapt to
the step function h╬╝ in Sect.3.4.
123


--- PAGE 17 ---
Estimation of spectral gaps for sparse symmetric matrices
863
Remark 3.7 If there is an eigenvalue of Tm inside the gap (╬╗ne(╬╝), ╬╗ne(╬╝)+1), the results
above do not hold. However, it is still possible to detect that there is a gap in the
spectrum of A. Indeed, let us assume that the gap (╬╗ne(╬╝), ╬╗ne(╬╝)+1) has relative width
╬╕ and that there is exactly one eigenvalue ╧ä of Tm in the gap. Then, the gap is split
into the two smaller gaps (╬╗ne(╬╝), ╧ä) and (╧ä, ╬╗ne(╬╝)+1), and one of them has a relative
width at least ╬╕/2. In other words, there is an interval of relative width ╬╕/2 where there
are no eigenvalues of A or Tm, and hence when ╬╝ is inside that interval the Lanczos
approximation error satis∩¼ües the bound (3.6) with ╬╕ replaced by ╬╕/2. In order to ensure
that this gap is detected, it is then enough to use ╬╕/2 instead of ╬╕ as a target gap width
for our algorithm. We remark that with this approach we are only able to detect the
larger portion of the gap, and not the whole gap in 	(A); however, the accuracy in
the approximation of the gap can be re∩¼üned for a small computational cost by simply
running a few extra Lanczos iterations, which would cause the eigenvalue of Tm to
move around the gap or, even better, to disappear from the gap.
3.4 An a posteriori error bound for the Lanczos algorithm
In this section we adapt an a posteriori error bound given in [26] for the approximation

formula and the residue theorem to obtain upper and lower bounds on the error in the
approximation of xT f (A)x with a rational Krylov method. This result can be easily
used to bound the error in the Lanczos approximation for f = h╬╝, but it requires
some caution since the function h╬╝ is discontinuous at ╬╝. In the following, we brie∩¼éy
retrace the proof of [26, Theorem 4.8] and adapt it to the current setting.

below Proposition 3.6.

The ΓÇ£central ╬╝ΓÇ¥ is the most accurate between the two a priori bounds, but it does not
capture the correct convergence rate for ╬╝ = 10.6, which is further from the middle
of the spectrum; in this case, a better a priori bound would be obtained by using
a convergence result with nonsymmetric intervals, such as [36, Theorem 1.1]. The
a posteriori bound is also quite irregular, but it is able to capture the convergence rate
of the Lanczos approximation correctly in both cases, although it is off by a constant
factor. Surprisingly, the error estimate |xT h╬╝(A)x ΓêÆqm| Γëê|qm ΓêÆqm+1| is very
accurate, and it seems to almost never underestimate the error. This behavior can be

4 Final algorithm
In this section we put together all the theoretical results obtained in Sect.3 to improve
Algorithm 1 and obtain an ef∩¼ücient algorithm that also provides some guarantees on
its output.
4.1 Choosing the input parameters
First of all, we need a criterion to decide the number s of Hutchinson sample vectors
and the number m of Lanczos iterations. For this purpose, given a failure probability
╬┤ and a target relative gap width ╬╕, we set the goal to ∩¼ünd all gaps in the spectrum of
A with relative width greater than or equal to ╬╕, with probability 1 ΓêÆ╬┤.
How should we choose s and m so that all these gaps are found? Let us consider a
candidate gap [╬╝1, ╬╝2] ΓèéR and ∩¼üx a parameter ╬╡ > 0, and say that we take m such
that the Lanczos approximation error for each quadratic form xT
i P╬╝xi is smaller than
╬╡/2 for all ╬╝ inside the gap, so that trH
s (P╬╝) is contained in an interval of width smaller
than ╬╡ for all ╬╝ Γêê[╬╝1, ╬╝2]. Then there are two possible scenarios: either there are
no eigenvalues of A in the interval [╬╝1, ╬╝2], or all the jumps in trH
s (P╬╝) associated to
eigenvalues inside that interval are ╬╡-small (more precisely, the sum of the heights of
these jumps is smaller than ╬╡). This second event has a small probability of occurring,
which can be bounded using Lemma 3.2, so we can conclude that [╬╝1, ╬╝2] is a gap in
the spectrum of A with high probability.
The probability that all jumps in trH
s (P╬╝) associated to eigenvalues in [╬╝1, ╬╝2]
are ╬╡-small is smaller than the probability of a single jump being ╬╡-small, so we can

We mention that bounds on the number of Hutchinson sample vectors required to
achieve a certain accuracy have been obtained in [15, 16] in the context of spectral
density estimation. For instance, it is shown in [16, Theorem 1] that, in order to obtain
an accuracy of order t on the normalized spectral density in the Wasserstein distance, it
is enough to take roughly

1/nt2
sample vectors. When t is not too small, this implies
that it is suf∩¼ücient to use a single vector in HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator, a result that matches
the conclusion reached in this section. However, note that the result from [16] is not
applicable in our setting: indeed, an absolute error ╬╡ on ne(╬╝) corresponds to an error
t = ╬╡/n on the normalized spectral density, so that the number of samples predicted by
[16, Theorem 1] would be roughly

n/╬╡2
, which grows linearly with n, matching our
initial observations in Sect.3.2. Another key difference is that the theoretical results
for spectral density estimation are usually given in terms of the Wasserstein distance
or some other global measure for the quality of approximation. On the other hand,
we are only interested in approximating the spectral density close to large gaps, so
it is reasonable to expect that better results can be obtained with a different kind of
analysis. We refer to Sect.4.5 for further discussion and comparison of our analysis
with the existing literature.
4.2 Detecting gaps in the spectrum
To detect the gaps in the spectrum, we use an a posteriori error bound to determine
when the error of the Lanczos approximation is smaller than ╬╡/2. We focus on the case
s = 1, with a single random Gaussian vector x ΓêêRn, since we concluded that it is the
most ef∩¼ücient choice in Sect.4.1. More precisely, for each ╬╝ we perform m iterations
of the Lanczos method to obtain qm(╬╝) ΓëêxT P╬╝x with the approximation (2.2), and
we use either Proposition 3.8 or the estimate based on consecutive differences to obtain

the a posteriori error bound from Proposition 3.8, and very often we can also expect it

4.3 Algorithm summary
The analysis in the previous sections leads to the following algorithm, whose pseu-
docode is given in Algorithm 2. Given an input failure probability ╬┤ and target relative
gap width ╬╕, we ∩¼ürst compute ╬╡ according to (4.2) such that the probability of having
an ╬╡-small jump at an eigenvalue in HutchinsonΓÇÖs trace estimator is less than ╬┤ when
using a single random vector (i.e., with s = 1). We then use Proposition 3.6 to select
123

M. Benzi et al.
Algorithm 2 Eigenvalue gap ∩¼ünder
Input: Symmetric matrix A ΓêêRn├ùn, failure probability ╬┤, target relative gap size ╬╕, vector of test param-
eters ╬╝ = [╬╝1, . . . , ╬╝N f ]
Output: tr j Γëêtr(P╬╝ j ) for j = 1, . . . , N f , intervals [ai, bi] that contain no eigenvalues of A with
probability 1 ΓêÆ╬┤ each
1: Compute ╬╡ according to (4.2), ensuring that the probability of a jump being smaller than ╬╡ with s = 1
Hutchinson vectors is smaller than ╬┤.

relative width at least ╬╕, so, assuming that the a posteriori error bound or estimate is
at least as accurate as the a priori one, we expect that all gaps of relative width ╬╕ or
larger are detected by Algorithm 2. Numerical evidence shows that this assumption
is generally satis∩¼üed when ╬╝ is not too close to the extrema of the gap and ╬╡ is not
too large. We also mention that, although Algorithm 2 cannot explicitly ∩¼ünd the gap
between the k-th and the (k +1)-th eigenvalue for a given k, it still computes estimates
for tr(P╬╝ j ) for all ╬╝ j Γêê╬╝, so we know the approximate number of eigenvalues below
each detected gap and thus we can roughly determine if one of the gaps found by
the algorithm is likely to be the one between ╬╗k and ╬╗k+1 (of course, we can only
expect that Algorithm 2 will detect this gap if we assume that it is wide enough). If
123


--- PAGE 25 ---
Estimation of spectral gaps for sparse symmetric matrices
871
more accuracy on the number of eigenvalues below each gap is required, then one
should use a larger number s of sample vectors for HutchinsonΓÇÖs trace estimator.
Alternatively, if it is feasible to compute an L DLT factorization, we can use a single
factorization to compute the exact number of eigenvalues below a certain gap in order
to verify if it corresponds to the searched gap between the eigenvalues ╬╗k and ╬╗k+1.
Observe that while computing such a factorization can be quite expensive, it would
still be signi∩¼ücantly cheaper than trying to ∩¼ünd the gap between ╬╗k and ╬╗k+1 by
factorizing shifted matrices AΓêÆ╬╝I in a trial-and-error approach, which would require
the computation of several L DLT factorizations.
Remark 4.1 Recall that xT P╬╝x is a staircase-like piecewise constant function of ╬╝,
and in particular it must be constant in any interval that contains no eigenvalues
of A. This means that given a candidate gap [╬╝1, ╬╝2], in addition to the condition
|qU
M(╬╝2) ΓêÆqL
M(╬╝1)| Γëñ╬╡, we would also need to check that qL
M(╬╝2) < qU
M(╬╝1). If
this additional condition is not satis∩¼üed, it is impossible for xT P╬╝x to be a constant
function for ╬╝ Γêê[╬╝1, ╬╝2], and hence we can conclude with certainty that there must be
at least one eigenvalue of A in the interval [╬╝1, ╬╝2]. This seems to cause a contradiction
when the condition |qU
M(╬╝2) ΓêÆqL
M(╬╝1)| Γëñ╬╡ is veri∩¼üed but qL
M(╬╝2) < qU
M(╬╝1) is
not, since it would imply that all the eigenvalues of A contained in [╬╝1, ╬╝2] have an
associated ╬╡-small jump, which is a low-probability event. However, it simply means
that this situation is very unlikely to happen in a practical scenario, and it would
probably not be encountered if the algorithm was run on the same problem for a second
time.Inourimplementation,wealsocheckiftheconditionqL

Remark 4.2 We mention that one could implement a variant of Algorithm 2 which does
not require as input a vector of parameters ╬╝ = [╬╝1, . . . , ╬╝N f ], but instead detects gaps
by sequentially evaluating the upper and lower boundsqU
M(╬╝) andqL
M(╬╝) on suitably
chosen points. Such an algorithm would likely employ fewer than N f values of ╬╝ to
reach the same accuracy. However, since the bulk of the computational cost is in the
construction of the Krylov subspace, the number of points N f has a negligible impact
on performance (see Sects.2.3 and 4.4) and thus we did not explore this approach in
detail, opting instead for a more straightforward strategy with a suf∩¼üciently dense grid
of points. We note that in order to detect all target gaps with our approach, N f must
be chosen so that there are at least two ╬╝ j that lie in each gap with width greater than
╬╕.
4.4 Computational cost
The most expensive operations in Algorithm 2 are the m matrixΓÇôvector products
with A required to construct the Krylov basis in the Lanczos algorithm, which cost
O(m nnz(A)) for a sparse matrix A. The additional operations done for the com-
putation of the quadratic forms xT P╬╝ j x with N f different parameters ╬╝ j add up to
O(nm + m2 + mN f ), as discussed in Sect.2.3. If we estimate the error with the con-
secutive difference estimate, we have to run the Lanczos algorithm for one additional
iteration, and compute the differences |qmΓÇ▓, j ΓêÆqmΓÇ▓+1, j| for all mΓÇ▓ ΓêêM. If |M| = d
123


--- PAGE 26 ---
872
M. Benzi et al.
and the elements of M are consecutive integers, this amounts to computing d + 1
quadratic forms qmΓÇ▓, j for all j, so the additional cost is O(m2d + mN f d).
On the other hand, if we use the a posteriori error bound from Proposition 3.8, we
have to evaluate the coef∩¼ücients ╬▒ j, ╬▓ j and ╬│ j for all ╬╝ j, which can be done with
O(m2) operations assuming that the eigenvalue decomposition of Tm is available, and
compute the maximum of |gm(z)| over [╬╗min, ╬╗max], where gm is de∩¼üned in (3.10). The
evaluation of gm(z) for all ╬╝ j at a single point z requires O(mN f ) operations, and if a
discretization of [╬╗min, ╬╗max] with L points is used, we can approximately evaluate the
bound in Proposition 3.8 for all ╬╝ j with O(m2 + mN f L) operations. If a set M with
d elements is used to compute the more robust boundsqU
M(╬╝ j) andqL
M(╬╝ j), the cost
for the computation of the bounds is roughly multiplied by a factor d and becomes
O(m2d + mN f Ld), where we have also included the cost of computing the eigen-
values and eigenvectors of TmΓÇ▓ for mΓÇ▓ ΓêêM. Note that given qU
m(╬╝ j) and qL
m(╬╝ j), the
re∩¼üned bounds qU
m(╬╝ j) and qL
m(╬╝ j) described in Sect.4.2 can be computed for all ╬╝ j
with O(N f ) operations. Finally, onceqU
M(╬╝ j) andqL
M(╬╝ j) have been computed, the
gaps can be located with a cost of O(N f ). The total computational cost of Algorithm
2 is therefore O(m nnz(A) + nm + m2d + mN f d) with the consecutive difference
estimate, and O(m nnz(A)+nm +m2d +mN f Ld) with the a posteriori bound from
Proposition 3.8.
Note that all operations associated with different values of ╬╝ are completely inde-
pendent, so the algorithm can be easily parallelized, for instance by assigning different
slices of the spectral interval to different processors. This parallelization is not par-
ticularly helpful when the matrix size n is large enough, since the dominant part of
the computation would still be the O(m nnz(A)) term associated with matrixΓÇôvector
products with A; however, parallelizing the computations associated with different
parameters ╬╝ can be helpful for mitigating the O(mN f Ld) cost of computing the a
posteriori error bounds from Proposition 3.8, which can become quite high if both N f
and L are large, see, for instance, the experiment in Sect.5.3.
If our goal is to detect all gaps with relative width larger than ╬╕ with probability at
least 1 ΓêÆ╬┤, we should take m according to (4.1) with ╬╡ ΓëñeΓêÆ1╬┤2, i.e.,

Although the dependence of m on the failure probability ╬┤ and the matrix size n is
not particularly severe since they appear inside a logarithm, the main drawback of
this approach is the O(1/╬╕) dependence on the relative gap width ╬╕, which causes
Algorithm 2 to quickly become more expensive as the target gap width is reduced. This
is an inherent limitation of the Lanczos method for the computation of xT P╬╝x, and
more precisely of the polynomial approximation of the sign function (see Proposition
3.3). As we mentioned in Sect.3.3, Proposition 3.3 can be improved by considering
two nonsymmetric intervals as in [36], and this would in turn lead to an improved
estimate on the required number of Lanczos iterations m; however, in general we do
not expect to ∩¼ünd an asymptotic estimate that is better than m = O(1/╬╕). Indeed, for
a gap located near the center of 	(A), the spectrum of A is contained in two intervals
that are approximately symmetric with respect to the gap, and there would not be any
signi∩¼ücant advantage in using the improved result [36, Theorem 1.1].

4.5 Literature comparison
As we already mentioned in some of the previous sections, the combination of Hutchin-
sonΓÇÖs trace estimator with the Lanczos algorithm has been used in several works on
related problems, for instance in [13, 15, 16]. The main aspect that sets our work apart
from the rest of the literature is the focus on the detection of gaps, and the consequent
difference in the theoretical analysis that we perform. Indeed, most results in the liter-
ature on spectral density approximation are concerned with global approximation of
the spectral density, such as in the Wasserstein distance [16, Theorem 1], so they are
unable to exploit the faster convergence of the Lanczos method for xT h╬╝(A)x when
╬╝ is located inside a large gap (see Proposition 3.6). Moreover, convergence results
found in the literature are usually based on the approximation error for tr(P╬╝) with
HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator, using results such as Theorem 2.1. In our context, we require
an absolute error on ne(╬╝) = tr(P╬╝) smaller than 1
2, which corresponds to a relative
error of 1
2n on the normalized spectral density; as we observed at the end of Sect.4.1,
achieving this level of accuracy with a bound such as [16, Theorem 1] would require
us to take s = O(n) vectors for HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator, which are too many to make
this approach feasible.
On the other hand, our analysis based on the height of the jumps in xT h╬╝(A)x and
trH
s (P╬╝) bypasses the need to obtain a highly accurate approximation of the trace and
allows us to use signi∩¼ücantly fewer matrixΓÇôvector products. The main tradeoff is that
we do not obtain the precise number of eigenvalues below each detected gap, but only
an estimate. To the best of our knowledge, this perspective and the resulting approach
for the theoretical analysis have not appeared before in the literature. Although we
have not explored this direction, it is possible that some of the techniques used in this
work may also be applicable to related research areas in which the combination of
Hutchinson and Lanczos is employed, such as spectral density estimation.
123


--- PAGE 29 ---
Estimation of spectral gaps for sparse symmetric matrices
875
5 Numerical experiments
In this section we run some experiments to investigate the performance of Algorithm
2. For all the experiments, we use Algorithm 2 with a failure probability ╬┤ = 10ΓêÆ2
and a number of Lanczos iterations m that is either ∩¼üxed in advance or computed
according to (4.5) in order to ∩¼ünd all gaps with relative width larger than a certain
target ╬╕. When not stated differently, for the Lanczos algorithm we employ the error
estimate boundm(╬╝ j) = c|qm, j ΓêÆqm, j+1| with a safety factor c = 2, and we use
the more robust upper and lower estimates qUΓïå(╬╝) and qLΓïå(╬╝) de∩¼üned in (4.6), with
M = {m ΓêÆd + 1, . . . , m} with d = 3. These estimates are cheaper to compute
compared to the a posteriori error bound from Proposition 3.8, and they are also quite
reliable, as shown in Example 4.1. Even though the estimate c|q j,m ΓêÆq j,m+1| does
not have the same theoretical guarantees as the a posteriori error bound, we have seen
in Example 3.2 that it is usually much closer to the actual error. All the experiments
have been done using MATLAB R2024b on a laptop running Ubuntu 24.04, with 32
GB of RAM and an Intel Core Ultra 9 185H CPU with base clock rate 2.3 GHz. The
code to reproduce the experiments in this section is available on Github at https://
github.com/simunec/eigenvalue-gap-∩¼ünder.
5.1 Scaling with the gap width
We start by examining the behavior of Algorithm 2 with respect to the relative gap
width ╬╕.
We consider a symmetric tridiagonal matrix A of size n = 30,000 obtained as a sum
A = D + T , where T is a random symmetric tridiagonal matrix with N(0, 1) entries
and D is a diagonal matrix. The eigenvalues of D are chosen so that the spectrum of
A has a gap with relative width approximately ╬╕. Speci∩¼ücally, the eigenvalues of D
are contained in the intervals [1, 103] Γê¬[103 + x, 104], where x satis∩¼ües
x/2
104 ΓêÆ103 ΓêÆx/2 = ╬╕,
so that the relative width of the gap [103, 103 + x] is ╬╕, and hence the spectrum of
the perturbed matrix A = D + T also has a gap with relative width approximately
╬╕. We consider a matrix D with 20,000 logspaced eigenvalues in [1, 103] and 10,000
logspaced eigenvalues in [103 + x, 104].
We estimate this gap using Algorithm 2, with m computed according to (4.5), so
that we can expect it to successfully ∩¼ünd all gaps with relative width larger than ╬╕;
we use N f = 10,000 parameters ╬╝, logarithmically spaced on the interval [1, 104].
For each ╬╕, in Table 1 we show the true gap computed by diagonalizing A, the gap
estimated by Algorithm 2 and the approximate number of eigenvalues below the gap,
obtained by rounding the trace approximation xT P╬╝x for ╬╝ equal to the left endpoint
of the estimated gap, as well as the number of Lanczos iterations m, the execution
time of the algorithm and the time required for the computation of the eigenvalues of
A. We see that the gap is found successfully for all ╬╕, with the estimated gap being
123


--- PAGE 30 ---
876
M. Benzi et al.
slightly larger than the true gap only for ╬╕ = 0.1 and ╬╕ = 0.05. The estimated number
of eigenvalues below the gap roughly approximates the exact value 20,000, but it
is still relatively far from the correct number since we are using only one sample
vector for HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator. Note that the number of Lanczos iterations scales
approximately as O(1/╬╕), but the execution time increases more than linearly in the
numberofLanczositerationsm;thisislikelyduetothe O(m2)costforthecomputation
of eigenvalues and eigenvectors of the projected matrix Tm becoming an increasingly
dominant part of the computation when the subspace dimension m is large. On the other
hand, the execution time for eig remains constant, so when the gap width ╬╕ becomes
suf∩¼üciently small it would be more ef∩¼ücient to directly diagonalize the matrix instead
of using Algorithm 2. Of course, this would not be the case if the matrix dimension n
increases, as we show in the following experiment.
5.2 Scaling with the matrix size
Let us now consider a sequence of matrices with increasing size and approximately
constant gap width. We use a setup similar to Sect.5.2, with ∩¼üxed relative gap width
╬╕ = 0.01 and increasing matrix size n. We take A = D + T , where T is symmetric
tridiagonal with random N(0, 1) entries and D is diagonal with 	(D) Γèé[1, 103] Γê¬
[103 + x, 104], with n/2 logspaced eigenvalues below the gap and n/2 logspaced
eigenvalues above the gap. The value of x is chosen so that A has relative gap width
approximately ╬╕, as in Sect.5.2.
We use Algorithm 2 to estimate the gap, with the number of Lanczos iterations
m computed according to (4.5) and N f = 10,000 parameters ╬╝, logarithmically
spaced over the interval [1, 104]. The results for a matrix dimension that increases
from n = 5000 to n = 80,000 are shown in Table 2. In this case, the gap is found
correctly for all matrix sizes, with none of the estimated gaps being larger than the
true gap computed via a diagonalization. Note that the number of Lanczos iterations
increases very slowly with the matrix dimension, in accordance with the discussion in
Sect.4.4. Hence, the execution time of the algorithm increases roughly linearly, since
with a large matrix size n and moderate subspace dimension m the leading term in the
computational cost is given by the m matrixΓÇôvector products with A in the construction
of the Lanczos basis, which cost O(mn) for a tridiagonal matrix. On the other hand,
the time required for computing the eigenvalues of the tridiagonal matrix A scales like
O(n2), so it quickly becomes more expensive than Algorithm 2. Note that if A was a
sparse matrix that is not tridiagonal, the diagonalization time would scale like O(n3),
making the difference between the two approaches even more evident.
It is interesting to observe that Algorithm 2 can be also used to cheaply obtain a
rough estimate of the gap by taking a larger failure probability ╬┤ and ∩¼üxing the number
of Lanczos iterations m in advance instead of computing it with (4.5). For instance,
we show in Table 3 the performance of Algorithm 2 with ╬┤ = 0.5 and m = 250. We
see that the gap in the spectrum is still located successfully, although the accuracy
is generally lower and sometimes the estimated gap is larger than the true gap, for
instance in the case n = 5000. Note that the estimated number of eigenvalues below
the gap coincides with the corresponding number from Table 2, even if the number

not to the error in the approximation of the quadratic form via the Lanczos algorithm.
5.3 Examples from applications
In this section, we demonstrate the effectiveness of Algorithm 2 on two illustrative
examples arising from practical applications.
5.3.1 Dirac comb Hamiltonian
As a ∩¼ürst model problem, we consider the one-dimensional discrete Hamiltonian oper-
ator
A = ΓêÆL + V ,
where L is a ∩¼ünite difference discretization of the second derivative on [0, N] with
periodic boundary conditions using a uniform grid with k points on each unit interval,
for a total of n = kN points, and V is a diagonal matrix representing a discrete Dirac
comb periodic potential, which takes the value k2 at the points x = 1, 2, . . . , N ΓêÆ1,
and is zero otherwise. This problem is a toy model that mimics the properties of a
Hamiltonian of a one-dimensional physical system with a periodic potential, whose
spectrum is typically characterized by several gaps, representing forbidden energy
levels for the electrons in the system; see for instance [3, Chap. 7] and [41] for more
details.
We use a discretization with N = 2000 and k = 5 for this example, for a total of
n = 10,000 discretization points. The spectrum of the resulting symmetric tridiagonal
matrix A ΓêêRn├ùn, shown in Fig. 4, has ∩¼üve bands, separated by four relatively large
gaps, each containing a single isolated eigenvalue, which splits it into two parts; we will
refer to them respectively as the lower and upper part of the gap. We shift and scale A
so that its spectrum is contained in the interval [0, 10], to facilitate visualization of the
gaps.WerunAlgorithm2todetectthegaps,bothwiththerobustconsecutivedifference
estimates(4.6)andwiththeerrorboundsfromProposition3.8,usingm = 150 Lanczos
iterations and d = 3. In Fig.5 we show the approximations to xT P╬╝x and the upper
and lower bounds obtained with the two approaches, where x is the single random
vector used in HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator; we use the same vector x for the two variants of
Algorithm 2. Note that the bounds from Proposition 3.8 are signi∩¼ücantly less accurate
than the estimates (4.6) in regions far from the gaps, but both approaches are extremely
accurate inside the gaps. With the chosen parameters, Algorithm 2 successfully detects
both parts of the ∩¼ürst gap, the lower half of the second and third gaps, and both parts
of the fourth gap; by increasing the number of Lanczos iterations to m = 250, all gaps
are successfully detected.

Fig. 4 Spectrum of the Dirac comb discrete Hamiltonian
In Table 4 we compare the exact gaps obtained by directly computing the eigen-
values of A with the approximate ones obtained with Algorithm 2, for the lower half
of the four gaps, along with the time required to run each method. Note that the gaps
found by using the a posteriori bounds from Proposition 3.8 are slightly smaller than
the ones obtained by estimating the Lanczos error with consecutive differences; this is
a consequence of the lower accuracy of the a posteriori bounds. Moreover, the execu-
tion time is quite higher with the a posteriori bounds, due to the O(mN f Ld) term in
the computation of the error bounds from Proposition 3.8; indeed, it turns out that this
is the most expensive part of the computation in this setting, where we use N f = 1000
different values of ╬╝ and a discretization with L = 1000 points to evaluate the maxi-
mum of |gm(z)| over the spectral interval of A. On the other hand, when the error in
the Lanczos algorithm is estimated using consecutive differences, the portion of the
computational cost that depends on N f scales as O(mN f d), which is signi∩¼ücantly less
expensive than the error bounds from Proposition 3.8 when L = 1000 (see Sect.4.4).
5.3.2 Hydrogen molecule chain
As an example from electronic structure computations, we consider the discretized
Hamiltonian associated with a linear chain of 1250H2 molecules. In contrast to the
atomic hydrogen chain, which is widely used as a theoretical benchmark but does not
occur in nature, the molecular chain corresponds to a physically realizable system
while still being straightforward to de∩¼üne; see, e.g., [42].
The matrices are generated with the open-source package PySCF [43], a Python
package for performing electronic structure computations using Density Functional
Theory. The code returns the Hamiltonian A, obtained through a Galerkin discretiza-
tion over a basis of Gaussian functions, and the overlap matrix S. Although the basis
123


--- PAGE 36 ---

M. Benzi et al.
Fig. 5 Approximations to xT P╬╝x, and upper and lower bounds obtained with Algorithm 2 for different ╬╝,
with m = 150 Lanczos iterations, for the problem in Sect.5.3.1. Left: Algorithm 2 with robust consecutive

functions have global support, the Hamiltonian and overlap matrices are approxi-
mately sparse: most entries decay rapidly with the distance from the main diagonal
and are set to zero once they fall below the threshold used for the integrals2. The basis
can be orthogonalized by taking the Cholesky factorization S = RT R, where S is
the overlap matrix. Thus, the new Hamiltonian is A = RΓêÆT ARΓêÆ1, and the energy
levels correspond to the eigenvalues of A; see [2, Section 3]. When the intermolecular
spacing in the chain is suf∩¼üciently large, the system behaves as a semiconductor [42],
i.e., the spectrum exhibits a gap between occupied and unoccupied states. To ensure
this, we impose an intermolecular distance of 2.5├à, while the bond length within the
H2 molecule is set to 0.74├à.
The test matrix A has size n = 5000. The spectrum, shown in Fig.6, has two
relatively large gaps and a smaller gap in the middle of the spectrum. There is a
single isolated eigenvalue within the third gap, splitting it into two parts. In electronic
structure computations, the gap of interest is the HOMO-LUMO gap, which in this
setting corresponds to the ∩¼ürst gap in Fig.6. The original Hamiltonian A and the
Cholesky factor R have 189,622 and 99,804 nonzero entries, respectively. Although
A is also approximately sparse, in order to compute the matrixΓÇôvector products needed
for the construction of the Krylov basis it is more computationally ef∩¼ücient to use the
representation A = RΓêÆT ARΓêÆ1 instead of forming A explicitly, so we rely on the
solution of the linear systems with R and RT , which are triangular and sparse, and on
multiplications with A.
In this test we use a setup similar to Sect.5.3.1. We ∩¼ünd the gaps in the spectrum
of A using Algorithm 2, and we compare the robust consecutive difference estimates
de∩¼üned in (4.6) with the a posteriori bound from Proposition 3.8, both obtained using

with the two approaches. We again observe that the upper and lower bounds obtained

6 Conclusions
We have described and analyzed an algorithm for identifying gaps in the spectrum
of a real symmetric matrix by simultaneously approximating the traces of spectral
projectors associated with different slices of its spectrum, combining HutchinsonΓÇÖs
stochastic trace estimator and the Lanczos algorithm. We have investigated the behav-
ior of HutchinsonΓÇÖs estimator, and provided a priori and a posteriori error bounds for
the Lanczos algorithm. This analysis led to an algorithm capable of detecting all gaps
larger than a speci∩¼üed threshold with high probability. We have also determined that
for this problem the best strategy is to use a single random sample vector for Hutchin-
sonΓÇÖs estimator, and utilize all the available matrixΓÇôvector products to approximate
a single quadratic form via the Lanczos algorithm. Our numerical tests demonstrate
that the developed algorithm can ef∩¼üciently and reliably detect gaps, especially when
their width is relatively large. Within our algorithm we have proposed to use either
an a posteriori error bound for the Lanczos approximation error ( Proposition 3.8), or
a simple error estimate based on consecutive differences. Although the a posteriori
error bound has better theoretical guarantees, the robust version of the error estimate
from Example 4.1 has more accurate results in practice, and it is also computationally
cheaper, notably when a large number of values of ╬╝ is used. Even when the algo-
rithm fails to detect a gap, it still provides upper and lower bounds for xT P╬╝x for all
considered values of ╬╝, which can be valuable for selecting a starting point for other
algorithms, such as those based on L DLT factorizations.

Γâ¥2016 Society for Industrial and Applied Mathematics
Vol. 37, No. 3, pp. 836ΓÇô860
COMPUTATION OF GENERALIZED MATRIX FUNCTIONSΓêù
FRANCESCA ARRIGOΓÇá, MICHELE BENZIΓÇí, AND CATERINA FENU┬º
Abstract. We develop numerical algorithms for the e∩¼âcient evaluation of quantities associated
with generalized matrix functions [J. B. Hawkins and A. Ben-Israel, Linear Multilinear Algebra, 1
(1973), pp. 163ΓÇô171]. Our algorithms are based on Gaussian quadrature and GolubΓÇôKahan bidiag-
onalization. Block variants are also investigated. Numerical experiments are performed to illustrate
the e∩¼Çectiveness and e∩¼âciency of our techniques in computing generalized matrix functions arising
in the analysis of networks.
Key words. generalized matrix functions, Gauss quadrature, GolubΓÇôKahan bidiagonalization,
network communicability
AMS subject classi∩¼ücations. 65F60, 15A16, 05C50
DOI. 10.1137/15M1049634
1. Introduction. Generalized matrix functions were ∩¼ürst introduced by Hawkins
and Ben-Israel in [17] in order to extend the notion of a matrix function to rectangular
matrices. Essentially, the de∩¼ünition is based on replacing the spectral decomposition
of A (or the Jordan canonical form if A is not diagonalizable) with the singular value
decomposition (SVD) and evaluating the function at the singular values of A, if de-
∩¼üned. While it is likely that this de∩¼ünition was inspired by the analogy between the
inverse and the MooreΓÇôPenrose generalized inverse, which is well established in nu-
merical linear algebra, [17] is a purely theoretical paper and does not mention any
potential applications or computational aspects. The paper (as well as the treatment
found in [5, section 6.7]) appears to have gone largely unnoticed, despite increasing
interest in matrix functions in the numerical linear algebra community over the past
several years.1 While it is likely that the perceived scarcity of applications is to blame
(at least in part) for this lack of attention, it turns out that generalized matrix func-
tions do have interesting applications and have actually occurred in the literature
without being recognized as such; see section 4 for some examples.
In this paper we revisit the topic of generalized matrix functions, with an empha-
sis on numerical aspects. After reviewing the necessary background and de∩¼ünitions,
we consider a few situations naturally leading to generalized matrix functions. Moving
on to numerical considerations, we develop several computational approaches based
on variants of GolubΓÇôKahan bidiagonalization to compute or estimate bilinear forms
involving generalized matrix functions, including entries of the generalized matrix
function itself and the action of a generalized matrix function on a vector. We fur-
ther consider block variants of GolubΓÇôKahan bidiagonalization which can be used to
evaluate matrix-valued expressions involving generalized matrix functions. Numeri-

1.1. A note on terminology. As mentioned in the introduction, the original
motivation for Hawkins and Ben-Israel appears to have been the desire to extend the
notion of a matrix function to rectangular matrices.
However, generalized matrix
functions are well de∩¼üned for any matrix A, as long as the underlying scalar function
is de∩¼üned on the singular values of A. Unfortunately, as observed in [18, page 353], the
notion of a generalized matrix function does not reduce (in general) to the standard
de∩¼ünition when A is a square matrix, and therefore the name is somewhat misleading.
This may be the reason the adjective ΓÇ£generalizedΓÇ¥ was dropped in the presentation
given in [5, section 6.7]. For lack of a better name, and in order to avoid confusion,
in this paper we have chosen to adhere to the terminology used in the original paper
[17].
2. Background. In this section we review a few basic concepts from linear
algebra that will be used throughout the paper, mostly to set our notation, and
recall the notion of a generalized matrix function.
Let A ΓêêCm├ùn, and let r be the rank of A.
We can factor the matrix A as
A = U╬úV Γêùusing an SVD. The matrix ╬ú ΓêêRm├ùn is diagonal, and its entries ╬úii = ╧âi
are ordered as ╧â1 ΓëÑ╧â2 ΓëÑ┬╖ ┬╖ ┬╖ ΓëÑ╧âr > ╧âr+1 = ┬╖ ┬╖ ┬╖ = ╧âq = 0, where q = min{m, n}. The
r positive ╧âi are the singular values of A. The matrices U = [u1, u2, . . . , um] ΓêêCm├ùm
and V = [v1, v2, . . . , vn] ΓêêCn├ùn are unitary and contain the left and right singular
vectors of A, respectively. It is well known that the matrix ╬ú is uniquely determined,
while U and V are not. If A is real, then U and V can be chosen to be real. From
the SVD of a matrix A it follows that AAΓêù= U╬ú╬úT U Γêùand AΓêùA = V ╬úT ╬úV Γêù. Thus,
the singular values of a matrix A are the square roots of the positive eigenvalues of
the matrix AAΓêùor AΓêùA. Moreover, the left singular vectors of A are the eigenvectors
of the matrix AAΓêù, while the right singular vectors are the eigenvectors of the matrix
AΓêùA. The singular values of a matrix also arise (together with their opposites) as the
eigenvalues of the Hermitian matrix

Remark 5. If f(0) = 0 and the matrix A ΓêêCn├ùn is Hermitian positive semidef-
inite, then the generalized matrix function f Γïä(A) reduces to the standard matrix
function f(A). If the more general decomposition of Theorem 3 is used instead, then
the generalized matrix function reduces to f(A) if f(0) = 0 and the matrix A ΓêêCn├ùn
is normal, as long as f is de∩¼üned on the ci; see [17].
Remark 6. The MooreΓÇôPenrose pseudoinverse of a matrix A ΓêêCm├ùn, denoted by
AΓÇá, can be expressed as f Γïä(AΓêù), where f(z) = zΓêÆ1. Equivalently, f Γïä(A) = (AΓÇá)Γêùwhen
f(z) = zΓêÆ1. Hence, there is a slight disconnect between the de∩¼ünition of a generalized
matrix function and that of a generalized inverse. This small issue could be addressed
by de∩¼üning a generalized matrix function corresponding to the scalar function f as
f Γïä(A) = Vrf(╬úr)U Γêù
r , so that the generalized matrix function of an m ├ù n matrix is
an n ├ù m matrix, and f Γïä(A) = AΓÇá when f(z) = zΓêÆ1. However, doing so would lead
to the undesirable property that f Γïä(A) = AΓêùfor f(z) = z, as well as other problems.

4. Manifestations of generalized matrix functions. As mentioned in the

few examples that we are aware of. No doubt there have been other such instances.

where the matrix in the upper right block is precisely ΓêÆsinΓïä(B). The authors of [10]
develop computational techniques for the matrix exponential based on (5). We also
mention that in the same paper the authors derive a similar expression, also found in
[6], for the exponential of the symmetric matrix A given in (1). These expressions are
extended to more general matrix functions in [22], where they are used to investigate
the o∩¼Ç-diagonal decay of analytic functions of large, sparse, skew-symmetric matrices.
Furthermore, in [11] it is shown how these ideas can be used to develop e∩¼âcient
geometrical integrators for the numerical solution of certain Hamiltonian di∩¼Çerential
systems.
In [8], the authors consider the problem of detecting (approximate) directed bi-

underlying graph leads them to introduce a ΓÇ£nonstandard matrix functionΓÇ¥ of the

As mentioned, generalized hyperbolic matrix functions were also considered in [6]
in the context of directed networks, also based on the notion of alternating walks in
directed graphs. In [2], the action of generalized matrix functions on a vector of all
ones was used to de∩¼üne certain centrality measures for nodes in directed graphs; here
the connection with the work of Hawkins and Ben-Israel was explicitly made.
Generalized matrix functions also arise when ∩¼ülter factors are used to regularize
discrete ill-posed problems; see, e.g., [16].
Several additional applications of generalized matrix functions are listed in the
recent paper [1], together with an extension to compact operators between in∩¼ünite-
dimensional Hilbert spaces. The authors of [1] appear to be unaware of the earlier
work by Hawkins and Ben-Israel.
5. Computational aspects. Hereafter we focus on the real case, both for sim-
plicity of exposition and because we are primarily interested in applications leading
to real matrices.
The computation of the generalized matrix functions de∩¼üned as in De∩¼ünition 4
requires the knowledge of the SVD of A. When m and n are large, computing the
SVD may be unfeasible. Moreover, in most applications it is not required to compute
the whole matrix f Γïä(A); rather, the goal is often to estimate quantities of the form
(6)
ZT f Γïä(A)W,
Z ΓêêRm├ùk, W ΓêêRn├ùk,
or to compute the action of the generalized matrix function on a set of k vectors,
i.e., to evaluate f Γïä(A)W, usually with k Γë¬min{m, n}.
For example, computing
selected columns of f Γïä(A) reduces to the evaluation of f Γïä(A)W where W consists of
the corresponding columns of the identity matrix In, and computing selected entries
of f Γïä(A) requires evaluating ZT f Γïä(A)W where Z contains selected columns of the
identity matrix Im.

The problem of estimating or giving bounds on such quantities can be tackled,
following [14], by using Gauss-type quadrature rules. As usual in the literature, we
will ∩¼ürst analyze the case k = 1; the case of k > 1 will be dealt with in section 6.
5.1. Complete monotonicity of the functions. It is known that in certain
cases Gauss-type quadrature rules can be used to obtain lower and upper bounds
on bilinear forms like zT f(A)w, where f(A) is a (standard) matrix function and
A = AT ΓêêRn├ùn. This is the case when f enjoys certain monotonicity properties.
Recall that a real-valued function f is completely monotonic (c.m.) on an interval
I = [a, b] if it is continuous on I and in∩¼ünitely di∩¼Çerentiable on (a, b) with
(ΓêÆ1)Γäôf (Γäô)(t) ΓëÑ0,
t Γêê(a, b)
ΓêÇΓäô= 0, 1, . . . ,
where f (Γäô) denotes the Γäôth derivative of f and f (0) = f. If f is c.m. on an interval
containing the spectrum of A = AT , then one can obtain lower and upper bounds on
quadratic forms of the type uT f(A)u, and from these one can obtain lower and upper
bounds on bilinear forms like zT f(A)w with z ╠╕= w. For a general f, on the other
hand, Gaussian quadrature can only provide estimates of these quantities.
Similarly, in order to obtain bounds (rather than mere estimates) for bilinear ex-
pressions involving generalized matrix functions, we need the scalar functions involved
in the computations to be c.m.
Remark 12. We will be applying our functions to diagonal matrices that contain
the singular values of the matrix of interest. Thus, in our framework, the interval on
which we want to study the complete monotonicity of the functions is I = (0, Γê₧).
We brie∩¼éy recall here a few properties of c.m. functions; see, e.g., [25, 28] and
references therein for systematic treatments of complete monotonicity.
Lemma 13. If f1 and f2 are c.m. functions on I, then
(i) ╬▒f1(t) + ╬▓f2(t) with ╬▒, ╬▓ ΓëÑ0 is c.m. on I;
(ii) f1(t)f2(t) is c.m. on I.

In the following, we propose three di∩¼Çerent approaches to approximating the
bilinear forms of interest. The ∩¼ürst approach exploits the results of Theorem 10 to
describe zT f Γïä(A)w as a bilinear form that involves standard matrix functions of a
tridiagonal matrix. The second approach works directly with the generalized matrix
function and the MooreΓÇôPenrose pseudoinverse of a bidiagonal matrix.
The third
approach ∩¼ürst approximates the action of a generalized matrix function on a vector
and then derives the approximation for the bilinear form of interest.

5.4. Third approach. Assume that we have used Γäô= r = rank(A) steps of the

The quality of the approximation will depend in general on the distribution of the
singular values of A and on the particular choice of f. Generally speaking, if f(╧âi) is
much larger on the ∩¼ürst few singular values of A than for the remaining ones, then a
small number of steps result in approximations with small relative errors.
6. The block case. In this section we describe two ways to compute approxi-
mations of quantities of the form (6) when k > 1. It is known that for this kind of
problem, block algorithms are generally more e∩¼âcient than the separate computation
of each individual entry (or column) of ZT f Γïä(A)W.
When dealing with blocks Z and W with a number of columns k > 1, the complete
monotonicity of the function f does not ensure that block Gauss-type quadrature
rules provide bounds on the quantities of the form ZT f Γïä(A)W. In this case, indeed,
no information about the sign of the quadrature error can be obtained from the
remainder formula for the Gauss quadrature rules [14]. Therefore, we focus on the
computation of approximations for the quantities of interest, rather than on bounds.
We propose two di∩¼Çerent approaches to computing the quantities (6). The ∩¼ürst one
exploits the connection between generalized matrix functions and standard matrix
functions described in Theorem 10, while the second one ∩¼ürst approximates the action
of a generalized matrix function on k vectors and then derives the approximation of
the quantities of interest.

Gauss and anti-Gauss quadrature rules [21, 7, 12] based on the nonsymmetric block
Lanczos algorithm [14]. As already pointed out, if we let g(t) = (

7. Numerical results. In this section we present some numerical results con-
cerning the application of the previously introduced techniques to the computation of
centrality and communicability indices in directed networks. The ∩¼ürst set of experi-
ments concerns the computation of the total hub communicability of nodes, which, for
a node i, is de∩¼üned as the following bilinear form:
(15)
C(i) := [hΓïä(A)1]i = eT
i hΓïä(A)1,
where h(t) = sinh(t), A is the adjacency matrix of the digraph, and 1 is the vec-
tor of all ones. As shown in [2], this quantity can be used to rank how important
node i is when regarded as a ΓÇ£hub,ΓÇ¥ i.e., as a broadcaster of information (analogous
quantities rank the nodes in order of their importance as ΓÇ£authorities,ΓÇ¥ i.e., receivers
of information).
The second set of experiments concerns the computation of the
resolvent-based communicability [6] between node i, playing the role of broadcaster
of information, and node j, acting as a receiver. The quantities of interest here have
the form [hΓïä(A)]ij = eT
i hΓïä(A)ej, where now h(t) = ╬▒t(1 ΓêÆ(╬▒t)2)ΓêÆ1 and ╬▒ Γêê(0, ╧âΓêÆ1
1 )
(with ╧â1 = ╧âmax(A)).
In all the tests we apply the approaches previously described and we use as stop-
ping criterion
(16)
RΓäô=

x(Γäô+1) ΓêÆx(Γäô)
x(Γäô)
 Γëñtol,
where tol is a ∩¼üxed tolerance and x(Γäô) represents the approximation to the bilinear
form of interest computed at step Γäôby the method under study. The relative error,
used to assess the accuracy of the approximation, is denoted by
EΓäô= |x(Γäô) ΓêÆzT hΓïä(A)w|

.
Our dataset contains the adjacency matrices associated with three real-world
unweighted and directed networks: Roget, SLASHDOT, and ITwiki [4, 9, 26]. The
adjacency matrix associated with Roget is 1022 ├ù 1022 and has 5075 nonzeros. The
graph contains information concerning the cross-references in RogetΓÇÖs Thesaurus. The
adjacency matrix associated with SLASHDOT is an 82168├ù82168 matrix with 948464
nonzeros. For this network, there is a connection from node i to node j if user i
indicated user j as a friend or a foe.
The last network used in the tests, ITwiki,
represents the Italian Wikipedia.
Its adjacency matrix is 49728 ├ù 49728 and has
941425 nonzeros, and there is a link from node i to node j in the graph if page i refers
to page j.

Comparison with standard Lanczos-based approach. In the case of gen-
eralized matrix functions like sinhΓïä(A), which occur as submatrices of ΓÇ£standardΓÇ¥
matrix functions applied to the symmetric matrix A , it is natural to compare the
previously proposed approaches with the use of Gauss quadrature-based bounds and
estimates based on the Lanczos process. This was the approach used, for example,
in [6]. Henceforth, we refer to this approach as ΓÇ£the mmq approach,ΓÇ¥ since it is imple-
mented on the basis of the mmq toolkit [24] originally developed by Meurant; see also
[14].
Numerical experiments, not shown here, indicate that on average, the mmq ap-
proach requires a slightly higher number of iterations than our third approach to de-
liver comparable accuracy in computing the communicability between pairs of nodes.
Note that the cost per step is comparable for the two methods. An advantage of
the mmq approach is that it can provide lower and upper bounds on the quantities
being computed, but only if bounds on the singular values of A are available.
A
disadvantage is that it requires working with vectors of length 2n instead of n.
Of course, the Lanczos-based approach is not applicable to generalized matrix

Block approaches. In the following, we test the performance of the two block
approaches described in section 6 when trying to approximate the communicabilities
among k nodes of the Twitter network, which has 3656 nodes and 188712 edges [27].
All the computations were carried out with MATLAB Version 7.10.0.499 (R2010a)
64-bit for Linux, in double precision arithmetic, on an Intel Core i5 computer with
4GB RAM.
In order to compute approximations to the communicabilities, we set
Z = W = [ei1, ei2, . . . , eik],
where i1, i2, . . . , ik are chosen uniformly at random among the n nodes of the network.
To better analyze the behavior of the methods, we run both algorithms ten times and
report in each table the averaged values obtained by changing the set of k nodes
after each run. As in the previous subsection, we test the performance of the two
generalized matrix functions induced by h(t) = sinh(t) and h(t) = ╬▒t(1 ΓêÆ(╬▒t)2)ΓêÆ1,
respectively.

GENERALIZED MATRIX FUNCTIONS
859
make any assumptions on the regularity of the function.
8. Conclusions. In this paper we have proposed several algorithms for the com-
putation of certain quantities associated with generalized matrix functions. These
techniques are based on Gaussian quadrature rules and di∩¼Çerent variants of the
Lanczos and GolubΓÇôKahan algorithms. In particular, we have investigated three dis-
tinct approaches to estimating scalar quantities like zTf Γïä(A)w and two block meth-
ods for computing matrix-valued expressions like ZT f Γïä(A)W. The performance of the
various approaches has been tested in the context of computations arising in network
theory. While not all methods can be expected to always perform well in practice,
we have identi∩¼üed two approaches (one scalar-based and the other block-based) that
produce fast and accurate approximations for the type of problems considered in this
paper.

our attention to the recent paper [1]. Francesca Arrigo and Caterina Fenu would like
to thank the Department of Mathematics and Computer Science of Emory University
for the hospitality o∩¼Çered in 2015, when part of this work was completed.
